  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_89c74a8a-6ba7-4fb7-9cad-fb792c57383c'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer9354534912483483476">Part One Of Two Parts<p>The author of THE FAR PAVILIONS returns us once again to the vast, intoxicating romance of India under the British Raj. SHADOW OF THE MOON is the story of Winter de Ballesteros, a beautiful English heiress come home to her beloved India.   <span id="freeText9354534912483483476" style="display:none">Part One Of Two Parts<p>The author of THE FAR PAVILIONS returns us once again to the vast, intoxicating romance of India under the British Raj. SHADOW OF THE MOON is the story of Winter de Ballesteros, a beautiful English heiress come home to her beloved India. It is also the tale of Captain Alex Randall, her protector, who aches to possess her. Forged in the fires of a war that threatens to topple an empire, their tale is the saga of a desperate and unforgettable love that consumes all in its thrall. Filled with the mystery of moonlit palace gardens and the whisperings of passion and intrigue, M. M. Kaye evokes an era at once of its time, yet timeless.</p><p>"Another splendid tale of India." (Wall Street Journal)</p></span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Unless someone has printed the novel in two parts, there are none. "Shadow of the Moon" is a solitary novel. It's not part of a series. Nor is it rela<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Unless someone has printed the novel in two parts, there are none. "Shadow of the Moon" is a solitary novel. It's not part of a series. Nor is it related to "The Far Pavilions", other than both novels were written by the same author.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<div id="review_852394115" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/852394115" />
    <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl"><img alt="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603085284p2/8734459.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/852394115?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl">Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Unknown Binding 9780312714109" href="/book/show/1124021.Shadow_of_the_Moon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=i-own">i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=india">india</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer852394115" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6331437567823772068">4.5 stars. <i>Shadow of the Moon</i> is a great historical epic, with love and hate, treachery and courage, set in India during the Sepoy Rebellion in the 1850s.<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1439699192i/15873821.jpg' width='400' height='200' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/>Lucknow, India<br/><br/>M.M. Kaye takes her time setting up the story, beginning it with the parents and grandparents of the main character, a half-English, half-Spanish girl with the unlikely name of Winter. Winter spends her first six years contentedly living in India, but when her parents die, she is sent to live with her English relatives. It's not </span>
  <span id="freeText6331437567823772068" style="display:none">4.5 stars. <i>Shadow of the Moon</i> is a great historical epic, with love and hate, treachery and courage, set in India during the Sepoy Rebellion in the 1850s.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1439699192i/15873821.jpg" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br>Lucknow, India<br><br>M.M. Kaye takes her time setting up the story, beginning it with the parents and grandparents of the main character, a half-English, half-Spanish girl with the unlikely name of Winter. Winter spends her first six years contentedly living in India, but when her parents die, she is sent to live with her English relatives. It's not a particularly happy situation for anyone involved, except for Winter's great-grandfather, the Earl of Ware and the patriarch of the family, who loves Winter. But Lord Ware is aging and frail, and so when there's a chance to betroth Winter (at age 11!!) to an older Englishman (in his 30's!!!) who's living in India and needs a (wealthy) bride, Winter and her great-grandfather both think that's a great idea, with plans to <s>seal the deal</s> marry the two once Winter comes of age.<br><br>Unfortunately, the intervening years don't do her betrothed any good: Conway Barton has always liked to party and sleep around, but by the time Winter is 17 he's a fat, lazy old drunkard (who still likes to party and sleep around). Rather than traveling to England himself, Conway sends for Winter to come to India and marry him there, figuring he can get her to tie the knot quickly, before she figures out what kind of a man he is, if she's alone and friendless. <br><br>Conway sends his young, handsome subordinate, Alex Randall, to escort Winter to India, which seems an odd and risky choice for Conway, but you have to roll with it. The meeting of Alex and 17 year old Winter does <u>not</u> go well; he feels honor-bound to try to warn her that she's marrying a fat, dissipated fool, and she can't <b>believe</b> he's bad-mouthing his superior officer and her fiancé like that! So off Winter goes to India, where a lecherous, depraved fiancé is only the first of the many problems and disappointments that she will be dealing with.<br><br>I got a little impatient with Winter in the first part of the book. She's so young and almost willfully blind, but you have to make allowances for a Victorian-era upbringing. And she grows up fast. Alex is a great character, intelligent and brave, loyal to his country but mightily struggling with short-sighted decisions that his superior officers are making about how to handle the volatile situation in India. Winter isn't the only one with blind spots.<br><br>M.M. Kaye doesn't pull any punches here, and the characters go through some really harrowing experiences. The death and mayhem during the Sepoy Rebellion is terrible, but overall historically accurate, and it's described in detail here. Between this book and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10222.The_Far_Pavilions" title="The Far Pavilions by M.M. Kaye" rel="nofollow noopener">The Far Pavilions</a>, Kaye's other epic about historic India, I've learned a lot that I had never known about India in the 1800's, and it's been fascinating.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1439699319i/15873827._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br>Indian Sepoys (infantry soldiers in the British East India Company army)<br><br>The ending is a bit abrupt; I would have liked an epilogue or something telling me a bit more about the fates of the characters after the fighting ended<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[, especially Lottie's baby! Does Lou keep her? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. I think <i>The Far Pavilions</i> is the better book of the two, by a thin margin, but I highly recommend both of these novels to anyone who enjoys historical fiction. Reading this reminded me of James Clavell's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/402093.Sh_gun__Asian_Saga___1_" title="Shōgun (Asian Saga, #1) by James Clavell" rel="nofollow noopener">Shōgun</a>, with fictional British characters in a foreign society with very different manners and ways. </["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="6331437567823772068" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Misfit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Misfit" href="/user/show/1375548-misfit">Misfit</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1375548-misfit?shelf=read-and-keep-for-a-reread">read-and-keep-for-a-reread</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer36960479" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8200242571131073098">Star crossed lovers, the British Raj & India, what more can you want in a book? This was just an amazing book. Once the author set up her characters and story line things just cooked along -- be prepared for the last 200 pages, because you will not surface for air until it's done! We have Winter, a wealthy heiress born and orphaned in India and sent to England to be raised by mostly uncaring relatives(except for the great-grandfather). When her great-grandfather dies, she is sent at the age of 1</span>
  <span id="freeText8200242571131073098" style="display:none">Star crossed lovers, the British Raj &amp; India, what more can you want in a book? This was just an amazing book. Once the author set up her characters and story line things just cooked along -- be prepared for the last 200 pages, because you will not surface for air until it's done! We have Winter, a wealthy heiress born and orphaned in India and sent to England to be raised by mostly uncaring relatives(except for the great-grandfather). When her great-grandfather dies, she is sent at the age of 17 to join her fiancee under the care of Alex Randall, who unbeknownst to her is now a debauched, obese drunk. Alex does try to tell her, but she maintains her childhood image of her "hero" and will not listen, to her great regret. <br /><br />Lots of trials and tribulations as our hero and heroine travel back to India, the meeting and marriage to Conway and the Sepoy rebellion, and vividly portrayed by an author who has a great knowledge and love of the country and it's history. This is not only a story of two lovers, but one of stubborn, bigoted officials hiding their heads in the sand, treachery, intrigue and the brutal way in which the rebellion played out against the British, even shocking some of their own people. As with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10222.The_Far_Pavilions" title="The Far Pavilions by M.M. Kaye" rel="nofollow noopener">The Far Pavilions</a>, it is shocking to see after 150 years not much of life and politics has changed in the Far East, nor should the Europeans (or Americans now for that matter) be interfering in their life, culture and religion. <br /><br />Highly recommended for any lover of historical fiction, India, or just a darn good book. This would make an awesome mini series, the sequences from the attack on the British and Alex and Winter's escape are just breathtaking. As a side note for those loooking for well written books for younger readers, this should be a good choice. Originally written in the 50s, the love scenes are quite chaste. Just be prepared for some gory, though accurate, portrayal of the violence aginst the British (including women and children) during the rebellion. <br /><br />If you enjoy this book, I would also recommend Zemindar. The same topic, the Sepoy rebellion, and beautifully written. The author's prose was gorgeous, very reminiscent of Charlotte Bronte. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8200242571131073098" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9571724008079247319">I can't do justice to a book like this. Thoughts and observations on an epic, sweeping saga can't be encapsulated into a 2-3 paragraph review saying how wonderful the characters, plot, writing, etc. was. Yes, I write these kind of reviews all the time, but for a book like this, it's virtually impossible.<br/><br/>So, I'll begin and end with this:<br/>If <i>Gone with the Wind</i> was Margaret Mitchell's love letter to the old South, then <i>Shadow of the Moon</i> is M.M. Kaye's to historic India. Under Kaye's pen, the unfold</span>
  <span id="freeText9571724008079247319" style="display:none">I can't do justice to a book like this. Thoughts and observations on an epic, sweeping saga can't be encapsulated into a 2-3 paragraph review saying how wonderful the characters, plot, writing, etc. was. Yes, I write these kind of reviews all the time, but for a book like this, it's virtually impossible.<br /><br />So, I'll begin and end with this:<br />If <i>Gone with the Wind</i> was Margaret Mitchell's love letter to the old South, then <i>Shadow of the Moon</i> is M.M. Kaye's to historic India. Under Kaye's pen, the unfolding beauty of India is revealed to the reader in slow increments, while the building tension leading up to the Sepoy Rebellion creates subtle unease for the reader. The rebellion itself is both equal parts horror and heroism, with the characters you've come to know and love taking more and more of your interest and sympathy. The last 200 pages are, literally, un-put-down-able. Schedule your reading time accordingly - you have been warned.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17954654660994370242">One of my all time favourite books! I fell in love with this novel as a 16 year-old and have spent the last nearly two DECADES trying to remember the name of it. Finally, thanks to Goodreads, I was able to dig up the title, and then I scored myself a treasured rare copy!<br/><br/>At last reunited with M.M. Kaye's epic masterpiece, I have been spellbound and enthralled for the last week, hating every minute spent away from my book. This tale of love, courage & survival set in India during the bloodthirsty </span>
  <span id="freeText17954654660994370242" style="display:none">One of my all time favourite books! I fell in love with this novel as a 16 year-old and have spent the last nearly two DECADES trying to remember the name of it. Finally, thanks to Goodreads, I was able to dig up the title, and then I scored myself a treasured rare copy!<br /><br />At last reunited with M.M. Kaye's epic masterpiece, I have been spellbound and enthralled for the last week, hating every minute spent away from my book. This tale of love, courage &amp; survival set in India during the bloodthirsty Sepoy Rebellion, is savage, heartbreaking and unashamedly romantic! (I'm a sucker for romance, but dislike bodice rippers, so M.M. Kaye's modest style is perfect.) Shadow of The Moon is richly plotted and decadently described, peopled with well-drawn and unforgettable characters, bolstered by an incredible depth of background detail and historical fact throughout, and with overriding suspense which builds to a gut-wrenching last 200 pages. Near impossible to put down once the mutiny begins! <br /><br />You just don't find historical writers like M.M. Kaye anymore. Or lovers like Winter and Alex, for that matter. Oh Alex! I'll leave the tweeny-boppers to fawn all over the pale sparkly Edward Cullen...give me Alex Randall any day! (Hey, he's a captain AND a midwife, what more can I say?!) And beautiful, resilient Winter -- I have long loved the image of her heart shaped face and blue-black hair.<br /><br />During my re-reading of this 1950's novel, I realized why it had appealed so much to me as a teen, and why it hasn't lost its charm for me! There is a dose of Austen in the drawing room dramas, lavish balls and the machinations of British society, shades of L M Montgomery in the avid, dreamy descriptions of sun rises and sets, and more than enough drama, intrigue, historical detail and throat-tightening suspense to enchant me entirely!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17954654660994370242" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_200311988" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/200311988" />
    <a title="Cindy Newton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6082517-cindy-newton"><img alt="Cindy Newton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1432075684p2/6082517.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/200311988?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cindy Newton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cindy Newton" href="/user/show/6082517-cindy-newton">Cindy Newton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Unknown Binding 9780312714109" href="/book/show/1124021.Shadow_of_the_Moon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6082517-cindy-newton?shelf=owned-books">owned-books</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer200311988" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer16906931131993484540">I love this book! It has been a favorite of mine for years, and this re-reading certainly hasn't changed that. M. M. Kaye spent a great deal of her life in India, and does (in my opinion) a powerful job of capturing the essence of that country--the sights, the sounds, the scents. You can feel the heat baking your skin, hear the noise and bustle of the colorful bazaar in the streets of the city, see the heat waves shimmering over the dusty streets, the exotic foliage and animals--it all comes ali</span>
  <span id="freeText16906931131993484540" style="display:none">I love this book! It has been a favorite of mine for years, and this re-reading certainly hasn't changed that. M. M. Kaye spent a great deal of her life in India, and does (in my opinion) a powerful job of capturing the essence of that country--the sights, the sounds, the scents. You can feel the heat baking your skin, hear the noise and bustle of the colorful bazaar in the streets of the city, see the heat waves shimmering over the dusty streets, the exotic foliage and animals--it all comes alive through her descriptive prose. Her prose, incidentally, sings; she's an artist with a sentence, and her words flow with effortless ease. But back to India. She creates a wonderfully vivid backdrop for the struggles of the characters--made even more compelling by the fact that the horrifying events actually took place. <br /><br />The main character, Winter, is a European woman whose connections to India are established through several generations at the beginning of the book. She is actually born there, and considers it her home. The male protagonist, Alex Randall, is a captain in the British army, and his love for India rivals Winter's. The story covers the build-up to and the events of The Sepoy Mutiny of 1857. A lot of background information is necessary in order to understand the bigger picture, but Kaye's coverage of this history is interesting, as she relates it with the clarity of hindsight, pointing out the bungling stupidity of those in charge and the many mistakes they made. These errors were glaringly apparent to a handful of men who actually loved and understood India, but their warnings were ignored and ridiculed. Alex Randall is a member of this group, and he fights desperately to avoid the impending disaster of the rebellion, but without success. The tension as you read of his efforts is excruciating, for you can see the writing on the wall.<br /><br />There is a romance, but it is very much in the background as the characters are swept into these dramatic events. There is a lot of food for thought in this book, for there are many issues that relate to our world today. Times change, customs change, the way we dress, talk, communicate, spend our time changes, but people don't change. We can read of this time and this place, and watch them making foolish assumptions and decisions, ignoring history and common sense, deceiving themselves with platitudes and excuses, and see the echoes of these behaviors in our leaders of today. <br /><br />I would highly recommend this book to anyone who loves historical fiction. When I read books like this, these sweeping epics that submerge you in another place and time, another culture, it is then that I can truly understand the magic of reading. I am no longer sitting on my couch in my comfortable suburban home--I am with them, with Winter and Alex on the hot, dusty plains, in the lush foliage of the jungle, in the brightly-painted rooms of the Gulab Mahal. I am suddenly immersed in the glamour and the squalor and the violence of this foreign country, and when I reach the end of the book, I'm still there. It takes a while to leave that exotic locale behind and return to my modern life. For a while, their images linger in my head, and the Hindustani words I learned while reading spring to mind automatically. I think of things from my normal life in the context of the book. This is the beauty of reading--how can <i>anybody</i> not love it??</span>
  <a data-text-id="16906931131993484540" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_205927744" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/205927744" />
    <a title="Angela Blount" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4269099-angela-blount"><img alt="Angela Blount" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592507072p2/4269099.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/205927744?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angela Blount" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angela Blount" href="/user/show/4269099-angela-blount">Angela Blount</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4269099-angela-blount?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4269099-angela-blount?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4269099-angela-blount?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4269099-angela-blount?shelf=some-kind-of-epic">some-kind-of-epic</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer205927744" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15019751617596719208"><br/>A sweeping romantic epic. Somewhat reminiscent of Wuthering Heights in its charming setting, rich descriptions... and in the use of weepy, waifish women as catalysts to the overarching plot. <br/><br/>While I spent the first half of the book having great difficulty sympathizing with the young, delusional, and allegedly enchanting protagonist, Winter, I felt compelled to keep reading for a number of reasons. Not the least of which involved a fascination with Captain Alex Randall. His staunch honor code was</span>
  <span id="freeText15019751617596719208" style="display:none"><br />A sweeping romantic epic. Somewhat reminiscent of Wuthering Heights in its charming setting, rich descriptions... and in the use of weepy, waifish women as catalysts to the overarching plot. <br /><br />While I spent the first half of the book having great difficulty sympathizing with the young, delusional, and allegedly enchanting protagonist, Winter, I felt compelled to keep reading for a number of reasons. Not the least of which involved a fascination with Captain Alex Randall. His staunch honor code was nearly as intriguing as his empathetic grasp of India's language and cultures. Winter eventually came around to become quite tolerable, but I read on and enjoyed because of Alex. <br /><br />The elegant, subtle prose didn't hurt, either. This is just one of those beautiful pieces of literature that feels as though it's seeping in and somehow improving my own capacity for literary expression. <br /><br />I can't say I was a big fan of the deep political aspects of the time period, nor of the bad combination of British arrogance and the oppressive caste system--resulting in the genocidal horrors that are eventually depicted. So much of the book felt like I was watching an impending train wreck in slow motion. It was obvious what was going to happen--as obvious as it was that my favored protagonist was fighting a futile battle trying to stop it. That frustration DID make it tedious at times. <br /><br />I got the sense that the story was essentially a history lesson designed to explain an actual event and the brutal retaliation that followed, and why it unfolded the way it did. But for that, I also have to congratulate the author. I learned a great deal about India, and about the motives of a number of different peoples during that time period. There were no easy answers, and it never felt preachy. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15019751617596719208" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_965318839" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/965318839" />
    <a title="Hana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10571559-hana"><img alt="Hana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1458172215p2/10571559.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/965318839?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hana" href="/user/show/10571559-hana">Hana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780517345924" href="/book/show/560540.Shadow_of_the_Moon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10571559-hana?shelf=18th-19th-century">18th-19th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10571559-hana?shelf=buddy-reads">buddy-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10571559-hana?shelf=2015-reads">2015-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10571559-hana?shelf=india-subcontinent">india-subcontinent</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10571559-hana?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer965318839" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15231059854319647027">Three and a half stars. Historical romance set in Hindustan during the Indian Rebellion of 1857. M.M. Kaye was born in Simla and lived much of her life in India. She was a close friend and mentor of Paul Scott whose magnificent novel, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/146746.The_Jewel_in_the_Crown__The_Raj_Quartet___1_' title='The Jewel in the Crown (The Raj Quartet, #1) by Paul Scott' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Jewel in the Crown</a>, covers the events leading up to Partition and Independence, some ninety years after the Rebellion and its bloody aftermath. M.M Kaye writes lyrically of India and her descriptive passages are splendid. Shadow of the Moon is also full of insi</span>
  <span id="freeText15231059854319647027" style="display:none">Three and a half stars. Historical romance set in Hindustan during the Indian Rebellion of 1857. M.M. Kaye was born in Simla and lived much of her life in India. She was a close friend and mentor of Paul Scott whose magnificent novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/146746.The_Jewel_in_the_Crown__The_Raj_Quartet___1_" title="The Jewel in the Crown (The Raj Quartet, #1) by Paul Scott" rel="nofollow noopener">The Jewel in the Crown</a>, covers the events leading up to Partition and Independence, some ninety years after the Rebellion and its bloody aftermath. M.M Kaye writes lyrically of India and her descriptive passages are splendid. Shadow of the Moon is also full of insights into the growing gulf between officials and military officers of the British East India Company and its sepoy troops. <br /><br />I finished two weeks ago and then took a vacation without writing my review. So here I am trying to remember the book and finding that very little stuck in my brain—never a good sign. Going back to my notes I had mixed feelings about the book even as I was reading it. Some parts were so well done and so exciting (the flight to the jungle, the bridge sabotage), but other parts dragged for me even though I know they were realistic (the long siege of Delhi, the lack of news from other areas). <br /> <br />The introductory chapters set during the Georgian and Regency period evoke the tolerant early days of the European adventure in India. All the complex intermarriages between Mughal royalty and European traders and adventurers are well captured in the family histories of the fictional de Ballesteros, Grantham and Mirza Ali Shah clans. The easy fusion of Hindu, Mughal and European traditions is very much on target. <br /><br />Unfortunately Kaye then embroils her young heroine, Winter de Ballesteros, in an extremely improbably series of disasters that uproot her from India and transplant her to England where she lives a Cinderella existence. Then, in an unlikely turn, Winter is betrothed as an 11 year old to a thirty year old fortune hunter making his way up the ladder in the East India Company. It’s all much too contrived and neither the situation nor Winter rang true for me. <br /><br />When Winter reaches her teens, her now dissolute betrothed demands that she be shipped out to India for a wedding that will put him firmly in charge of her fortune. Kaye really almost lost me at this point. There are way too many awkward plot twists and holes. Winter’s fiancé, Conway Barton, now in charge of a fictional Company district is too much a cardboard caricature of every deeply stupid British functionary, but it was kind of fun hating him and I was delighted with his ultimate <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ignominious drunken demise. Alas he was not killed off soon enough and we and Winter have to put up with him for rather longer than I would have liked. But the unfortunate marriage did allow for Winter to do some rapid, much needed growing up.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> .<br /><br />Fortunately, just as I was about to give up, Kaye shifted the scene and introduced a hero--young Alex Randall, Conway Barton’s assistant who basically runs the province while Barton drinks and plays cards with assorted Company low-life. Alex is darkly handsome with grey eyes, fluent in Hindustani, able to pass as a Pathan, with a loyal Muslim friend....Yes, yes, I know--clichés--and that is exactly the problem. Alex serves as one of those too-knowing prophets who decry the growing British greed and cultural and religious insensitivity and see the rebellion coming with 20-20 foresight. There really were a few men like Alex but it felt just a little too pat. Still, Alex is a hunk and very likable, and his adventures and the Alex-Winter scenes were fun and kept me going.<br /> <br />It sounds as if I should have given this a two star rating but Kaye kept me turning the pages and the sections that were pure historical narrative were well done and largely accurate (except for a Kali-sacrifice scene right out of Indiana Jones and the Temple of Doom). A map would have been helpful, along with an afterword explaining what was fiction and what was based on fact.<br /> <br />Those who like romantic adventures with a serious historical bent will enjoy this saga. Those seeking a more literary treatment of the British Raj should definitely try <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3119.Paul_Scott" title="Paul Scott" rel="nofollow noopener">Paul Scott</a>'s much more subtle <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/146746.The_Jewel_in_the_Crown__The_Raj_Quartet___1_" title="The Jewel in the Crown (The Raj Quartet, #1) by Paul Scott" rel="nofollow noopener">The Jewel in the Crown</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1000718.The_Day_of_the_Scorpion__The_Raj_Quartet___2_" title="The Day of the Scorpion (The Raj Quartet, #2) by Paul Scott" rel="nofollow noopener">The Day of the Scorpion</a>. For an excellent non-fiction treatments of the period covered by this book don’t miss <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/60159.William_Dalrymple" title="William Dalrymple" rel="nofollow noopener">William Dalrymple</a>'s superb <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/124432.White_Mughals_Love_and_Betrayal_in_Eighteenth_Century_India" title="White Mughals Love and Betrayal in Eighteenth-Century India by William Dalrymple" rel="nofollow noopener">White Mughals: Love and Betrayal in Eighteenth-Century India</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/124429.The_Last_Mughal_The_Fall_of_a_Dynasty_Delhi__1857" title="The Last Mughal The Fall of a Dynasty Delhi, 1857 by William Dalrymple" rel="nofollow noopener">The Last Mughal: The Fall of a Dynasty: Delhi, 1857</a>. Buddy read with a wonderful group of Goodreads friends: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/12044813-sotm-group-read---general">https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/...</a><br /><br />Content warning: PG for marital rape; intense scenes of massacres, beheadings, war atrocities, etc. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15231059854319647027" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Unknown Binding 9780312714109" href="/book/show/1124021.Shadow_of_the_Moon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21426381-dorcas?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21426381-dorcas?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21426381-dorcas?shelf=favorite-reads">favorite-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21426381-dorcas?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21426381-dorcas?shelf=mmkaye">mmkaye</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer727343666" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8856036475143216540">Some reviewers call this book a love story. But that is such a small part of it unless you want to classify the love of India as part of that love story. In which case, yes, it is most definitely a love story. But as far as romance, it's there, but is not the crux. First and foremost, this is a story of the uprising of India against the feringhi (foreigners) and here lies the turmoil: on which side does one belong when one belongs on neither side and yet both? And can one survive it?<br/><br/>I found this</span>
  <span id="freeText8856036475143216540" style="display:none">Some reviewers call this book a love story. But that is such a small part of it unless you want to classify the love of India as part of that love story. In which case, yes, it is most definitely a love story. But as far as romance, it's there, but is not the crux. First and foremost, this is a story of the uprising of India against the feringhi (foreigners) and here lies the turmoil: on which side does one belong when one belongs on neither side and yet both? And can one survive it?<br /><br />I found this an engrossing read and yet not a very cheerful one. The writing is 5 star but I found it rather dark and depressing. So many people die... I'm not someone who has to have a HEA (and this story does end well in case you're wondering) but I DO need some light moments, SOME happiness. And there was very little. Even the sweet moments were bitter-sweet. There are times when you want to yell at the book, "NOOOOO"!!!! Still, it's such a good story....<br /><br />Anyway,if you're looking for a realistic story of the Indian uprising written with amazing skill you will love this. The characters have depth and there are so many twists and turns which make for an exciting read. BUT, if you want a happy little fairy tale that gives you pleasant dreams at night look elsewhere.<br /><br />CONTENT:<br /><br />SEX: There's a sex scene which covers one small paragraph. It is not graphic by any means but in my opinion it is not suitable for YA readers.<br /><br />LANGUAGE: Mild to moderate depending on sensitivity.The 'N' word is used a few times (spoken by people who were in no way heroes), some B's and D's.<br /><br />VIOLENCE: This is a story of mutiny and there are massacres. Some is NOT pleasant reading. While not as graphic as some that writers of today produce, there ARE some brutal moments which may give you nightmares. Again,not recommended for YA audiences. However, this was what really happened and I'm pretty sure that the author used a light hand in relating the revolts. The reality was far worse.<br /><br />PARANORMAL ELEMENTS: The reader is led to believe at two points in the book that the heroine sees a ghost. However, this is never delved into and either confirmed or denied.<br />Also an epileptic child of one of the servants is believed by the natives to possess the "second sight" This is never confirmed, but apparently was a common belief when people were epileptic or had mental issues. <br /><br />MY RATING: A strong PG-13<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8856036475143216540" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780517345924" href="/book/show/560540.Shadow_of_the_Moon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1632609-anna?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1632609-anna?shelf=3-stars">3-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1632609-anna?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer458651822" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3882676732723476771">This book is mostly set in India as the 19th century Sepoy Rebellion against the British gathers pace and attacks with devastating results.<br/><br/>I enjoyed parts of it to a 4 star level, particularly Sabrina’s heartbreaking story, and Winter’s neglected childhood in England, her long journey back to India, her new life there with the awful Conway, and her dramatic escape as the Rebellion took savage hold.<br/><br/>So why only a 3 star rating? There was plenty of ‘he thought, she thought’ between our would-be-lov</span>
  <span id="freeText3882676732723476771" style="display:none">This book is mostly set in India as the 19th century Sepoy Rebellion against the British gathers pace and attacks with devastating results.<br /><br />I enjoyed parts of it to a 4 star level, particularly Sabrina’s heartbreaking story, and Winter’s neglected childhood in England, her long journey back to India, her new life there with the awful Conway, and her dramatic escape as the Rebellion took savage hold.<br /><br />So why only a 3 star rating? There was plenty of ‘he thought, she thought’ between our would-be-lovers Winter and Alex, but hardly any dialogue. This lack of direct interaction between them meant there was a lack of direct interaction between them and me, and I didn’t feel any emotional pull towards them.<br /><br />This was also the case with the supporting roles; the lack of conversations meant I felt I was being <i>told</i> the story rather than <i>living it</i> with them. I was shocked by the brutality of the Rebellion, but because I didn’t have a bond with the characters, I wasn’t moved when they fell. In such a tense and distressing time, I needed to <i>feel</i> the story, but sadly didn’t.<br /><br />I enjoyed the vibrancy of the Indian settings, as well as learning about life in 19th century India, an era I’m new to. I’m interested in reading more books set against this beautiful but dangerous backdrop, and hope that other books by Kaye, or by other authors, can make me feel more connected and involved, sweeping me up in true epic style.<br /><br />(eek, sorry Hannah and Misfit)<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3882676732723476771" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_151516549" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/151516549" />
    <a title="Rachel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3491697-rachel"><img alt="Rachel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1288322472p2/3491697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/151516549?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel" href="/user/show/3491697-rachel">Rachel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer151516549" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1725527771451694691">When I first read "Shadow of the Moon" as a young girl freshly enamored of M M Kaye's writing and my first encounter with her work in "The Far Pavilions". I was both disappointed and relieved at the reduced scale. Although it weighs in at 400+ pages (depending on your addition) it does not touch the level of geo-political rhetoric that Far Pavilions some times fell into. But with characters that are just as strong and clearly developed it is a much more, to me at any rate, enjoyable read. <br/>I must</span>
  <span id="freeText1725527771451694691" style="display:none">When I first read "Shadow of the Moon" as a young girl freshly enamored of M M Kaye's writing and my first encounter with her work in "The Far Pavilions". I was both disappointed and relieved at the reduced scale. Although it weighs in at 400+ pages (depending on your addition) it does not touch the level of geo-political rhetoric that Far Pavilions some times fell into. But with characters that are just as strong and clearly developed it is a much more, to me at any rate, enjoyable read. <br />I must confess that I do find the main character Alex to be infinitely more dreamy than Ash and Anjuli while more beautiful is also less relatable than Winter. And who doesn't love a heroine named Winter, seriously? But there I end my comparison as they are both great stories told by a great writer in their own rights. <br />I first read this as a stop gap in my knowledge of the vast and teeming sub-continent that is its setting. But if there is one thing that I do respect about M.M. Kaye for all her colonialist pedigree she doesn't play the role of the Westerner that comes to solve all of your problems. Nor does she go as far as E.M Forester ( what's with the Brit's and initial names?) in his one an only work set in India not so subtly titled, "A Passage to India" which was widely praised as progressive for subversively favoring the Indian plight. Which it may have indeed been when first released. Honestly, I think Forester sacrificed both plot and character development for the sake of his "message" which over the years became so obscured as to be indistinguishable to the uninitiated modern reader. Shadow of the Moon on the other hand never feels over wrought with message but neither does it shirk its task of presenting the story of a deeply troubled political time in a complicated country. Nor does she present the novel as an explanation or exculpation of either nation here at odd ends of a gun barrel but rather she sought to present it through the lens of human experience and as such it takes my breath away still and is my favorite historical novel set in the Indian Raj, which is not a category as obscure as it sounds.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1725527771451694691" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer859754162" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11226455762144767847">I have very mixed feelings on finishing this book; on one hand, I liked the storyline with all its adventures, twists and turns, which overall made for an enjoyable read and often not easy to predict, for Kaye has a decent mastery of storytelling that keeps you interested despite the length of the novel. And then, there's the characterisation of the protagonist, who's one of the most interesting leading male characters I've encountered in historical fiction recently, with flashes that reminded m</span>
  <span id="freeText11226455762144767847" style="display:none">I have very mixed feelings on finishing this book; on one hand, I liked the storyline with all its adventures, twists and turns, which overall made for an enjoyable read and often not easy to predict, for Kaye has a decent mastery of storytelling that keeps you interested despite the length of the novel. And then, there's the characterisation of the protagonist, who's one of the most interesting leading male characters I've encountered in historical fiction recently, with flashes that reminded me of another favourite, Ashton Pelham-Martyn, also created by this author for another of her works. He's very well fleshed out and compelling, and for me personally kept me reading at points where I felt I was in danger of losing interest in the narrative. <br /><br />But, on the other hand, there's the female character and her storyline. Her characterisation made her a very frustrating protagonist to read about most of the time, and at least a couple of times I found myself wishing that the leading female would be someone else instead of her. And when you catch yourself wishing such things, then it means the chemistry between characters was lost on you. <br /><br /><br /> </span>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18033200" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17752072199102855196">i don't care if there's cheesy bits, i must've read this book through at least eight times, and always pick it up to look at on the rare occasion when i can't sleep. set in colonial india, there's riots and history and violence and, yes, a ridiculously romantic main plot. i am not ashamed. i love this book.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2179118027" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2179118027" />
    <a title="Judy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3333943-judy"><img alt="Judy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267037102p2/3333943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2179118027?book_show_action=true">Nov 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Judy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Judy" href="/user/show/3333943-judy">Judy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=20th-century-fiction">20th-century-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=books-from-1956">books-from-1956</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3333943-judy?shelf=set-in-india">set-in-india</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2179118027" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11820410247125055459"> Over two decades ago I devoured M M Kaye's <i>The Far Pavillions</i> and <i>Trade Wind</i>. She writes the kind of long books I love: so readable and so historically instructive. Some months ago my blogger friend Helen reviewed <i>Shadow of the Moon</i> on her excellent historical fiction blog, She Reads Novels, reminding me I had missed this one. <br/> <br/>After I read <i>The Ministry of Utmost Happiness</i> by Arundhati Roy, I was inspired to learn more about the history of India. I was still too timid to try Salman Rushdie's <i>Mi</i></span>
  <span id="freeText11820410247125055459" style="display:none"> Over two decades ago I devoured M M Kaye's <i>The Far Pavillions</i> and <i>Trade Wind</i>. She writes the kind of long books I love: so readable and so historically instructive. Some months ago my blogger friend Helen reviewed <i>Shadow of the Moon</i> on her excellent historical fiction blog, She Reads Novels, reminding me I had missed this one. <br /> <br />After I read <i>The Ministry of Utmost Happiness</i> by Arundhati Roy, I was inspired to learn more about the history of India. I was still too timid to try Salman Rushdie's <i>Midnight's Children</i> again after a failed attempt to read it some years ago, so <i>Shadow of the Moon</i> seemed just the thing. It was!<br /><br />The British East India Company is in the waning years of its heyday. It is about to morph into the British Raj when its power to rule India passed to Queen Victoria after an uprising that nearly bankrupted the world's most powerful trading company.<br /><br />Through the eyes of Winter de Ballesteros, half British heiress and half Spanish Condessa, this novel tells the story of the rebellion by the Sepoys, the Indian infantry soldiers in the British East India Company army. I really got the sense of how much and for how long India was under the power of the British: for over 250 years under the East India Company and then another 73 years under the British Raj before achieving independence in 1947.<br /><br />The story goes deeply into the results and discontents of misrule. It is actually astonishing how much political history Ms Kaye covers in a novel that reads like a historical romance.<br /><br />Winter is a typical heroine for novels of this kind. She is naive and romantic, but strong and brave. After a life of sorrows and losses, she gets her happy ending. Life for women in India was difficult in the extreme. Both English and Indian women suffered in many ways, lost babies, died from disease, and had virtually no rights. Winter can be a frustrating character but considering all the trials she survived from the day of her birth, she became a beloved heroine for me.<br /><br />I admired how well M M Kaye captured that aspect of womanhood where no matter how brave, smart and resilient a woman was, she was forever being left behind to endure pregnancy, childbirth and early motherhood while her husband took off to settle business and political troubles. It's enough to make me want to watch Wonder Woman over and over!<br /><br />I loved the book. It took me seven days to read 274 pages of Susan Sontag but only four days to read 799 pages by M M Kaye. I do need both novels of ideas and those with propulsive storytelling in my reading life, but Ms Kaye combined the two so seamlessly. Now I actually feel sufficiently girded to tackle <i>Midnight's Children</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11820410247125055459" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1549548972?book_show_action=true">Feb 15, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nikki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nikki" href="/user/show/45951585-nikki">Nikki</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1549548972" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7779022741398271927">Oh my goodness! Where or where do I start?? First off, M. M. Kaye being my favorite writer of all times I literally have no idea how after all these years I have not read this book or The Far Pavillions or Trade Wind. Seriously how did I miss them? All in all, I'm actually so glad I did because now I have more books to look forward to reading! So... I downloaded the samples of all three books and was going to read each sample to decide which book I felt like reading first but I ended up only rea</span>
  <span id="freeText7779022741398271927" style="display:none">Oh my goodness! Where or where do I start?? First off, M. M. Kaye being my favorite writer of all times I literally have no idea how after all these years I have not read this book or The Far Pavillions or Trade Wind. Seriously how did I miss them? All in all, I'm actually so glad I did because now I have more books to look forward to reading! So... I downloaded the samples of all three books and was going to read each sample to decide which book I felt like reading first but I ended up only reading the sample for Shadow of the Moon because once I read the sample I had to know exactly what was going to happen in it! It pulled me in from the beginning.. I will say it starts out with the history of Winter's grandparents and then her parents and I while I definitely had to know what happened to each character I did find myself ready to get on with the story of Winter herself and Captain Randall. So, while interesting to see how Winter eventually ended up being brought back to England the beginning of the book is slightly tedious and slow moving. But! Do not stop reading!! By the time Captain Randall has escorted Winter to her betrothed( who was a horrible terrible man whom Randall had tried warning her against) you are dedicated at that point to the two main characters and are beginning to know the secondary characters as well. It was probley at this point in the book, that I found myself staying up way to late at night reading and finding myself thinking about these characters quite often during the day. Winter herself as some other reviewers have mentioned did take some time for me to actually like her. She was at times quite childish and immature but in reality she was just 17 so for the most part she was still just a child. When I found I began to care for her character is when she truly began to start growing up. It was a slow process but then all of a sudden you have realized our little Winter has become a strong woman. Alex Randall, I loved from almost the beginning. The entire book is leading up to the sepoy mutiny, it was almost like watching a shipwreck about to happen.. Frustrating so much.. And when it happened, it was awful... I was actually in some shock at what I was reading and had to fight the urge to not skip as to not know who lived or died but found I could not do that for I was to invested in the characters at this point... I loved every minute of this book after probley the first 200 pages... I do wish the ending hadn't been as swift and abrupt as it was.. All I can say is I will give it a small time and then probley read it all over again!!!!</span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8683688432569988730">A group of older friends I had when I was 16 introduced me to M.M. Kaye. The first book of hers I read was Shadow of the Moon. Due to school commitments I think it took me almost a week and I hated every moment I wasn't with this book. <br/><br/>From the very beginning this book enchanted me. It is filled with the very essence of India and its people. For years Alex was the epitome of everything that was perfect in a man- in some ways he still is. He does, at any rate, remain one of my all time favorites </span>
  <span id="freeText8683688432569988730" style="display:none">A group of older friends I had when I was 16 introduced me to M.M. Kaye. The first book of hers I read was Shadow of the Moon. Due to school commitments I think it took me almost a week and I hated every moment I wasn't with this book. <br /><br />From the very beginning this book enchanted me. It is filled with the very essence of India and its people. For years Alex was the epitome of everything that was perfect in a man- in some ways he still is. He does, at any rate, remain one of my all time favorites heroes. <br /><br />Winter is such a strong character, but so human. What could've been a one dimensional character evolved into, to me, an icon. No matter how many times I read this story I can not help but feel everything she does. I know what is going to happen and yet am still on the edge of my seat. <br /><br />This book is out of print, but you can find copies of it on amazon and ebay. If you enjoy epic romances this is so the book for you! Action, romance, exotic locales, murder and tears...it has it all. I also recommend Far Pavilions as a good follow up.</span>

<div id="review_1338782265" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1338782265" />
    <a title="Danielle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22995699-danielle"><img alt="Danielle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1432245867p2/22995699.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1338782265?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Danielle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Danielle" href="/user/show/22995699-danielle">Danielle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Unknown Binding 9780312714109" href="/book/show/1124021.Shadow_of_the_Moon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22995699-danielle?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22995699-danielle?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22995699-danielle?shelf=india">india</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1338782265" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6707013241592024163">It's strange how you can read two books that are very similar, but enjoy the poorer-quality one more than the better book. <br/><br/>After finishing Kaye's masterpiece <i>The Far Pavilions</i>, I had an incredible urge to read another one of her books, which definitely did not disappoint. I actually went into this book with much lower expectations, since many of my friends gave it so-so reviews, and I knew that this book was far less popular than the <i>The Far Pavilions</i>, and honestly I can see why. <i>The Far Pavilio</i></span>
  <span id="freeText6707013241592024163" style="display:none">It's strange how you can read two books that are very similar, but enjoy the poorer-quality one more than the better book. <br /><br />After finishing Kaye's masterpiece <i>The Far Pavilions</i>, I had an incredible urge to read another one of her books, which definitely did not disappoint. I actually went into this book with much lower expectations, since many of my friends gave it so-so reviews, and I knew that this book was far less popular than the <i>The Far Pavilions</i>, and honestly I can see why. <i>The Far Pavilions</i> truly is a masterpiece. It is an incredibly well-researched and well-written historical novel that is engaging, touching, and several times kept me on the edge of my seat. <i>Shadow of the Moon</i> is <i>also</i> all of those things, but to a slightly lesser degree. There were a few lines (not many) that I have to admit that were a tad too harlequin-romanceesque for my taste in particular. Also this book is much shorter, not to mention that the stakes seemed higher in the <i>The Far Pavilions</i> with the forbidden love story and hair-raising chills of that book. <br /><br />Despite this, I actually think I enjoyed <i>Shadow on the Moon</i> more. For one thing, it took me almost a year to read <i>The Far Pavilions</i>, which must mean something. I loved that book, but it is quite dense. On the other hand, this book took me five days and I was up late at night reading it, the first time in a while I enjoyed a book so much that it made me lose sleep. Also, as I mentioned in my review of <i>The Far Pavilions</i>, I think that one flaw of that book was that the the last couple of hundred pages describing the Afghan war dragged a little bit, which was not a fault of her writing but more that it is difficult to make a story seem interesting after the hair-raising climax that came before it. What I really like about this book was that the climax, the pacing and the ending all felt like they were right where they should be. <br /><br />One of the things that makes Kaye such a good writer is that she is a master of characterization. The two leads in the <i>The Far Pavilions</i> and this book are quite different, and she really seems to pay attention to the ages of the characters that she writes. For instance, I found the main character Winter to be a little naive and annoying at places, but I was willing to let it pass because Kaye made several points of mentioning how young (17!) and unexperienced in the harsh ways of the world that she was. By contrast, Anjuli of <i>The Far Pavilions</i> is a few years older (21 I think) and has endured so much that it has given her a more rational head on her shoulders. And in the same way the male leads of those two books are extremely different, in part due to their difference in ages. It worked well for me with this book, but it backfired a little bit with <i>The Far Pavilions</i>. I liked Ash in the <i>The Far Pavilions</i>, but he was an extremely rash and emotional character who would sometimes do things that were so stupid they made me want to yell at him. Again, I think he was in his early-twenties so in addition to his hot-headed nature his age would have compounded some of his decisions. Realistic, but frustrating for me as a reader. On the other hand, I absolutely fell in love with Captain Randall in this book. Alex is apparently in his early thirties and is shown to be a much-wiser, cooler and so unemotional almost to the point of robotic-ness person. He makes for a far less frustrating read than Ash does, bless his heart. <br /><br />But to reiterate, I <i>loved</i> both books. Reading these novels makes me wonder why people keep rebooting crappy movies that weren't made that long ago when they could make a beautiful movie based off this amazing book, which as far as I know has never been adapted?!<br /><br />I want to move on soon to her next book <i>Trade Wind</i>, but the reviews for that book are the worst of her three historical epics. I hope the chances that I am pleasantly surprised hold out some more. <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6707013241592024163" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Jane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2679341-jane"><img alt="Jane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442635072p2/2679341.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2044831747?book_show_action=true">Jun 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jane" href="/user/show/2679341-jane">Jane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/34456408-shadow-of-the-moon">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2044831747" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15346287135040205767">My five stars is for the story, not for the narration. The narrator mispronounced quite a lot of words in English, and her pronunciation of some of the Indian words was suspect even to my unknowledgeable ear. Her Indian accents were better than the English ones (she's American) which were all over the place, grating against my English antennae for speech distinctions; the effect was that the characters' class and even their regional origin seemed to be constantly shifting, which was disconcertin</span>
  <span id="freeText15346287135040205767" style="display:none">My five stars is for the story, not for the narration. The narrator mispronounced quite a lot of words in English, and her pronunciation of some of the Indian words was suspect even to my unknowledgeable ear. Her Indian accents were better than the English ones (she's American) which were all over the place, grating against my English antennae for speech distinctions; the effect was that the characters' class and even their regional origin seemed to be constantly shifting, which was disconcerting. American listeners may not notice this aspect of the narration.<br /><br />Great story, of course, because this is M.M. Kaye and you can expect high drama, improbable situations, narrow escapes, and heroic action. Star-crossed lovers Winter and Alex aren't one hundred percent likable (another Kaye trait) but they have innate nobility in spades. Naturally both are able to pass as Indians and have a deep love and understanding for the country and its people, while still retaining the best bits of Britishness, i.e. the aforementioned nobility, courage, endurance, etc. The story is set firmly during the Indian Mutiny and doesn't flinch from describing the horrors thereof, although a lot of hero Alex's story line is offstage and he spends an inordinate time on his sickbed during exactly the moment when you'd expect him to be off fighting--mostly, I suspect, to give one of the plot lines its day in the sun.<br /><br />The plot takes FOREVER to get going because you get at least two generations of backstory at the beginning, although all that setup really adds to the richness of the later chapters. Still, all that backstory could probably have been cut and woven into the main story, as most authors would do today.<br /><br />This novel dates from the 1950s so if you're going to read it you have to be prepared for some decidedly colonial attitudes and racist language. And lots of tropes that are now literary clichés, and probably were even at the time of writing--but they find their way into so many books because they work in terms of keeping the reader entertained.<br /><br />And I was tremendously entertained, despite the story's many flaws. Not quite as compelling as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10222.The_Far_Pavilions" title="The Far Pavilions by M.M. Kaye" rel="nofollow noopener">The Far Pavilions</a> but I enjoyed the historical background more in this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15346287135040205767" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14687652375350349322">I need to stop reading all this regressive, colonialist garbage. What can I say? I have been on a big M. M. Kaye thing recently. I guess I need to read "Far Pavilions" and just commit to the whole endeavor.<br/><br/>Anyhow, I really have a love-hate relationship with this stuff. She was raised in India and clearly has a deep love and understanding of the people, landscape, and culture. You can learn an incredible amount about colonial India by reading her stuff. <br/><br/>Having said that, the "romance" in this one</span>
  <span id="freeText14687652375350349322" style="display:none">I need to stop reading all this regressive, colonialist garbage. What can I say? I have been on a big M. M. Kaye thing recently. I guess I need to read "Far Pavilions" and just commit to the whole endeavor.<br /><br />Anyhow, I really have a love-hate relationship with this stuff. She was raised in India and clearly has a deep love and understanding of the people, landscape, and culture. You can learn an incredible amount about colonial India by reading her stuff. <br /><br />Having said that, the "romance" in this one (an extremely understated relationship between a British officer and a titled Anglo-Spanish heiress, is just ever-so-slightly unsettling because it a) takes place between the European characters b) who are, of course, uber-educated and, in her case, rich and titled, and c) just seems like a typical Western romance juxtaposed over a cool story about an important historical event, almost like an afterthought.<br /><br />I want to shake her and say, "You know, if you want to write a book about Colonial India and the Sepoy Rebellion, just do it. Don't feel like you have to slap a romance on top of it."<br /><br />Except that still wouldn't be a perfect solution because for all of her obvious love for this country and meticulous detail, that colonialist attitude still slips out. You know, "life is cheap in India" and that kind of thing.<br /><br />Anyhow, I'm trying to be honest about what I read (even if it's crap) and I read this. All 600 plus pages. It could be worse. At least Alex didn't rape Winter. (see Trade Winds)</span>

  <span id="freeText2005958018745464328" style="display:none">~ 4.5~<br /><b>A Heartfelt read.</b><br />The introduction, actually the first part, felt long; the story about the heroines' parents and their parents and generally the back story of the character's and the time period. Personally, I thought it an essential element to the rest of the story. <br /><br />Both main leads were interesting, the heroine more emotionally-driven than the down to earth-minded hero but nevertheless both found their <i>way to my heart.</i><br /><b>From the beginning to the end, I had this cool feeling that I'm reading something epic. </b><br /><br />The book is heavy for the content it has; the political situation, terror and because every fate of the character has a mixed happiness/sadness and tragedy in itself.<br /><br />I read it in a week but I must say the last part was really, as most said, unputdownable. It has many actions and just everything to process.<br /><b>Recommend when you have more time to process the book for its lengthiness and complicity.</b><br /><br />Warning!: you will come across many unpleasantries.</span>
